<span class="italic"><a title="Link to journal home page" href="https://doi.org/10.1039/2040-3372/2009">Nanoscale</a></span>, 2024, <strong>16</strong>, 4578-4590</div><h1 id="sect176"><span class="title_heading">Trace topological doping strategy and deep learning to reveal high-rate sodium storage regulation of barium-doped Na<small><sub>3</sub></small>V<small><sub>2</sub></small>(PO<small><sub>4</sub></small>)<small><sub>3</sub></small><a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a></span></h1><p class="header_text">

      28th August 2023
    </span><span class="bold italic">, Accepted 15th January 2024</span></div><p class="bold italic">First published on 24th January 2024</p><hr><div class="abstract"><h2>Abstract</h2><p>The urgent development of sodium ion batteries has stimulated the rapid innovation of sodium super ionic conductor-type Na<small><sub>3</sub></small>V<small><sub>2</sub></small>(PO<small><sub>4</sub></small>)<small><sub>3</sub></small> materials with high energy density and ultra-high charge/discharge rates, where the bottlenecks are the activation of multi-electron reactions and the utilization of the third sodium ion. Herein, we design a trace topological doping strategy to introduce barium ions into crystal domains of Na<small><sub>3</sub></small>V<small><sub>2</sub></small>(PO<small><sub>4</sub></small>)<small><sub>3</sub></small> to partially replace vanadium sites. Deep learning demonstrates that the violation of the inversion symmetry of vanadium by barium substitution can improve the structural stability and change the charge density distribution of vanadium, resulting in the re-distribution of surface electrons and supplying more possible migration paths for sodium ions. Simultaneously, the slight alteration of the crystal structure helps the positive shift of vanadium valence from +3 to +4, providing more multi-electron redox reactions. Among these candidates, NVBP-2 manifests a specific capacity of 65.1 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> at 50C rate with superior charge–discharge capability and cycling performance. Moreover, it possesses decent long-term cycling stability with 81.2% capacity retention after 2000 cycles at 50C. In summary, the results indicate that trace topological doping of alkaline metal ions in combination with deep learning has a novel ability to achieve sodium ion storage regulation for sodium ion batteries, which exquisitely provides a new perspective for screening cathode materials with high electrochemical performance.</p></div><hr>
    
      
      <h2 id="sect270"><span class="a_heading">1. Introduction</span></h2>
      <span>With fossil fuel depletion and rapid increase in energy demand, tremendous research efforts have focused on the development of renewable energy systems with green resources, powerful energy storage and less environmental pollution. Sodium ion batteries (SIBs), as one of the promising alternative energy storage systems, have attracted ever-increasing attention due to their low cost, environmental benignity, and inexhaustible resources.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit1"><sup><span class="sup_ref">1–5</span></sup></a> The low temperature charging ability of SIBs is superior to that of lithium-ion batteries, endowing them with huge promise as energy storage systems in the corresponding large scale energy storage field. Simultaneously, aluminum foil can be used as a substrate for cathode and anode during electrode preparation, further reducing the cost of SIBs and providing a huge price advantage. However, SIBs still suffer from sluggish kinetics as well as unsatisfactory electrochemical energy storage efficiency, due to the relatively larger sodium ion radius (Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>, 1.02 Å) when compared with its lithium counterpart (Li<small><sup>+</sup></small>, 0.76 Å). To date, various cathode materials like layered oxides (NaMnO<small><sub>2</sub></small>,<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit6"><sup><span class="sup_ref">6–8</span></sup></a> NaNi<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>Fe<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>Mn<small><sub>1/3</sub></small>O<small><sub>2</sub></small><img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]"><a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit9"><sup><span class="sup_ref">9,10</span></sup></a>), phosphates (NaFePO<small><sub>4</sub></small>,<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit11"><sup><span class="sup_ref">11,12</span></sup></a> Na<small><sub>2</sub></small>FePO<small><sub>4</sub></small>F,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit13"><sup><span class="sup_ref">13</span></sup></a> Na<small><sub>3</sub></small>Fe<small><sub>2</sub></small>(PO<small><sub>4</sub></small>)<small><sub>2</sub></small>P<small><sub>2</sub></small>O<small><sub>7</sub></small><img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]"><a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit14"><sup><span class="sup_ref">14</span></sup></a>), sulfides (Na<small><sub>2</sub></small>Fe(SO<small><sub>4</sub></small>)<small><sub>2</sub></small><img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]"><a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit15"><sup><span class="sup_ref">15,16</span></sup></a>), Prussian blue analogues<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit17"><sup><span class="sup_ref">17–19</span></sup></a> and organic series candidates<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit20"><sup><span class="sup_ref">20,21</span></sup></a> have been widely investigated in SIBs.</span>
      <p class="otherpara">Among many candidates, polyanion-type cathode materials with a Na super ionic conductor (NASICON) structure are of significant interest, as their unique 3D open framework endows these materials with rapid Na ion diffusion rates, as well as superior reversibility.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit22"><sup><span class="sup_ref">22,23</span></sup></a> In particular, Na<small><sub>3</sub></small>V<small><sub>2</sub></small>(PO<small><sub>4</sub></small>)<small><sub>3</sub></small> (NVP) has been identified as a potential cathode candidate due to its high ionic conductivity, low volume alteration, pronounced structural stability, and high safety.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit24"><sup><span class="sup_ref">24</span></sup></a> Unfortunately, the utilization of each vanadium atom in the V<small><sup>3+</sup></small>/V<small><sup>4+</sup></small> redox couple is limited by the intrinsically low electrical conductivity. To date, many efforts have been made to enhance the utilization ratio of V<small><sup>3+</sup></small>/V<small><sup>4+</sup></small> in NVP, including surface carbon coating,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit25"><sup><span class="sup_ref">25</span></sup></a> nano-crystallization,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit26"><sup><span class="sup_ref">26</span></sup></a> and heteroatom doping (for example, Nd,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit27"><sup><span class="sup_ref">27</span></sup></a> Ga,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit28"><sup><span class="sup_ref">28</span></sup></a> Mo,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit29"><sup><span class="sup_ref">29</span></sup></a> Ti,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit30"><sup><span class="sup_ref">30</span></sup></a> and Cl<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit31"><sup><span class="sup_ref">31</span></sup></a>). It is known that the combination of heteroatom doping and surface carbon coating is an efficient route to simultaneously increase the material intrinsic conductivity and surface conductivity without consuming much tap density.</p>
      <p class="otherpara">However, using a material data analysis approach is still rare for doping trace alkaline ions to achieve superior electrochemical performance and reveal the inner regulation of improved properties. Herein, trace barium (Ba) doping in NVP is presented in this work and evaluated as a cathode material for SIBs. By revealing the Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> migration route regulation and adsorption energy using topological symmetry analysis, in combination with the characterization of the Na storage mechanism by X-ray diffraction pattern (XRD), X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS), and related electrochemical analysis, the role of Ba-doping is systematically investigated. The results of theoretical calculations coincide with the experimental data, confirming that the electrochemical properties of the Ba-doped NVP exhibit excellent rate capability and significantly improved ultra-long cycling stability.</p>
    
    
      
      <h2 id="sect322"><span class="a_heading">2. Results and discussion</span></h2>
      <span>
        <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1(a)</a> shows the XRD patterns of Ba-doped NVP materials. For convenience, Ba-doped NVP with different doping amounts can be abbreviated as NVBP-<span class="italic">X</span>, where <span class="italic">X</span> = 1, 2, 4 and 8 represents Ba-doping amounts of <span class="italic">x</span> = 0.01, 0.02, 0.04 and 0.08, respectively. From the results, indexed peaks can be well matched with the rhombohedral NVP phase with <span class="italic">R</span><small><sub>3<span class="italic">c</span></sub></small> space group (PDF # 70-3613), indicating that the partial substitution of Ba (≤0.1) does not change the crystal structure of NVP. The detailed alteration of the (113) diffraction peak from the XRD pattern is shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1(b)</a>. With the increase of the Ba-doping amount to 0.01, there is a slight negative shift of (113) (<span class="italic">hkl</span>) towards a smaller diffraction angle. When the doping amount is further increased to 0.02, a significant negative shift of the (113) peak is observed. This change of the diffraction peak is attributed to the larger size of the Ba ion radius (+2, 1.35 Å) than the V ion radius (+3, 0.64 Å, in 6-fold coordination), suggesting the expansion of the unit cell volume upon Ba<small><sup>2+</sup></small> doping. However, when the doping amount increases to 0.04 and 0.08, the negative shift phenomenon of the selected peak disappears, which may be attributed to un-doping resulting from excessive doping amount. It is believed that the enlarged unit cell space can provide broader channels for Na ion diffusion, decreasing the Na ion migration energy and facilitating the electrode reaction kinetics. In addition, according to the Bragg formula, the interlayer space of the (113) peak in NVP can expand by substitution of V<small><sup>3+</sup></small> by Ba<small><sup>2+</sup></small> due to the bigger radius of Ba<small><sup>2+</sup></small> than V<small><sup>3+</sup></small>, which broadens the Na ion migration diffusion channel and benefits in achieving a superior high rate capability.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit32"><sup><span class="sup_ref">32</span></sup></a></span>
      <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig1"><a href="/image/article/2024/NR/d3nr04300b/d3nr04300b-f1_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d3nr04300b-f1.tif" src="/image/article/2024/NR/d3nr04300b/d3nr04300b-f1.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 1 </b> <span id="fig1"><span class="graphic_title">XRD patterns of NVP and NVBP-X materials: (a) full spectra and (b) partially expanded spectra.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      <p class="otherpara">
        <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2(a–e)</a> shows the scanning electron microscope (SEM) images of the prepared materials. As displayed in the images, all five materials are composed of particles with various shapes and sizes, even though there are some observably agglomerated particles with the size of 2–3 μm for NVP. It is observed that the agglomeration degree is much weaker in NVBP-X than in NVP. Additionally, the particle size decreases with increasing Ba-doping content. When the Ba-doping amount varies from 0.01 to 0.08, the size of NVP particles decreases from several micrometers to hundreds of nanometers, which facilitates electrolyte penetration and shortens the diffusion route of Na ions. Simultaneously, the relatively smaller particle size after Ba-doping could weaken the ionic scattering during electrochemical sodiation/de-sodiation, resulting in improved kinetic characteristics. <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2(f)</a> presents NVBP-2 and corresponding energy dispersive spectrometer (EDS) elemental mapping images. From the results, C, Na, V, P and O elements are detected, and Ba is dispersed uniformly in the composite, implying the homogenous elemental distribution of the material.</p>
      <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig2"><a href="/image/article/2024/NR/d3nr04300b/d3nr04300b-f2_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d3nr04300b-f2.tif" src="/image/article/2024/NR/d3nr04300b/d3nr04300b-f2.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 2 </b> <span id="fig2"><span class="graphic_title">SEM images of (a) NVP and (b–e) NVBP-X materials. (f) Selected sections from NVBP-2 and corresponding element mapping images from EDS.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      <p class="otherpara">To further elucidate the architectural morphological features and the structure of the carbon coating layer, transmission electron microscopy (TEM) measurement was carried out, and the results are shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3</a>. For NVP (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3(a–c)</a>), the image clearly reveals severely aggregated particles with the size ranging from 2 to 3 μm, matching well with the SEM observation. Moreover, the graphene wrinkles as well as amorphous carbon architecture with a thickness of about 2–3 nm are detected in accompaniment with crystalline NVP (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3(b and c)</a>). The residual carbon colony would serve as a high speed electron transfer network.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit33"><sup><span class="sup_ref">33</span></sup></a> Clear lattice fringes are detected with the lattice spacing of 0.374 nm, matching well with the (113) plane of the rhombohedral structure of NVP.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit34"><sup><span class="sup_ref">34</span></sup></a> After Ba-doping, as shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3(d–f)</a>, graphene sheets with an amorphous carbon architecture are still visible. The addition of Ba-based reagent slightly alters the crystal space of NVP since a larger lattice spacing (0.424 nm) is detected for NVBP-2. Simultaneously, the graphene layer with a lattice spacing of 0.34 nm is observable.</p>
      <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig3"><a href="/image/article/2024/NR/d3nr04300b/d3nr04300b-f3_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d3nr04300b-f3.tif" src="/image/article/2024/NR/d3nr04300b/d3nr04300b-f3.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 3 </b> <span id="fig3"><span class="graphic_title">TEM images of (a and b) NVP and (d and e) NVBP-2. HRTEM images of (c) NVP and (f) NVBP-2.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      <p class="otherpara">To further identify the carbon architecture of the five samples, the Raman spectrum and TG plot were characterized. As shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4</a>, it is verified that all five samples demonstrate two main characteristic bands of sp<small><sup>2</sup></small>-type carbon, including one band at 1590 cm<small><sup>−1</sup></small> and another band at 1350 cm<small><sup>−1</sup></small>. The former carbon band is attributed to the breakage of symmetry at the edges of the graphite sheet (the E<small><sub>2g</sub></small> vibration mode), known as the D band, while the later carbon band is ascribed to the A<small><sub>1g</sub></small> vibration mode, arising from the graphite band and known as the G band.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit35"><sup><span class="sup_ref">35,36</span></sup></a> From the analysis of the <span class="italic">I</span><small><sub>D</sub></small>/<span class="italic">I</span><small><sub>G</sub></small> value, NVP has a stronger peak intensity ratio of the D band than that of the G band in comparison with NVBP-X materials, suggesting that the graphitization degree of carbon in NVP may have been slightly changed by Ba-doping. This indicates that the Ba ion may have a faintly catalytic influence for graphitization of carbon, such as Ni, Co, and Fe,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit37"><sup><span class="sup_ref">37</span></sup></a> which is still under investigation. It is noted that there is a small peak in NVBP-X and NVP materials at 437 cm<small><sup>−1</sup></small>, which is ascribed to the existence of VO<small><sub>6</sub></small> or PO<small><sub>4</sub></small>.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit27"><sup><span class="sup_ref">27</span></sup></a> Besides, according to the thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) plot in Fig. S1,<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a> the relative weight ratio of carbon is 5.7% for NVBP-2.</p>
      <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig4"><a href="/image/article/2024/NR/d3nr04300b/d3nr04300b-f4_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d3nr04300b-f4.tif" src="/image/article/2024/NR/d3nr04300b/d3nr04300b-f4.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 4 </b> <span id="fig4"><span class="graphic_title">Raman spectrum of NVP and NVBP-X materials.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      <p class="otherpara">X-ray photoelectron spectrum (XPS) measurement was carried out to analyze the valence information of C, V and Ba in NVP and NVBP-2, and the results are presented in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5</a>. In <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5(a)</a>, the C 1s spectrum in NVP is composed of four carbon-based bonding peaks: O–C<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_e001.gif" alt="[double bond, length as m-dash]">O, C<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_e001.gif" alt="[double bond, length as m-dash]">O, C–O and C–C peaks located at 288.5, 286.5, 285.1 and 284.1 eV binding energies, respectively. After Ba-doping, C<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_e001.gif" alt="[double bond, length as m-dash]">O and C–O peaks shift slightly towards lower binding energy, whereas the C–C peak remains unchanged, suggesting that the carbon chemical environment is basically unaltered. For V 2p spectra, the high resolution spectra of NVP and NVBP-2 are shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5(c) and (d)</a>. Two de-convoluted peaks at 524.0 eV and 517.0 eV are attributed to V 2p<small><sup>3/2</sup></small> and V 2p<small><sup>1/2</sup></small> of V<small><sup>4+</sup></small>, respectively, while two others at 523.2 eV and 516.2 eV match well with V 2p<small><sup>1/2</sup></small> and V 2p<small><sup>3/2</sup></small> of V<small><sup>3+</sup></small>, respectively. The high ratio of V 2p<small><sup>3/2</sup></small> at around 517.0 eV can be ascribed to the higher chemical state of vanadium from +3 to +4, originating from the balance of the valence and the conservation of the charge from aliovalent acceptor doping of Ba<small><sup>2+</sup></small> for V<small><sup>3+</sup></small>.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit38"><sup><span class="sup_ref">38</span></sup></a> Moreover, from the analysis in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5(e) and (f)</a>, it is demonstrated that there is an emerging small characteristic peak existing at around 779.8 eV and 795.1 eV, which can be attributed to Ba<small><sup>2+</sup></small> 3d<small><sub>5/2</sub></small> and Ba<small><sup>2+</sup></small> 3d<small><sub>3/2</sub></small>.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit39"><sup><span class="sup_ref">39</span></sup></a> The detailed alteration of Ba<small><sup>2+</sup></small> before/after Na storage has been shown in Fig. S2.<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a> In the image, compared with the discharge state at 2.5 V, there is a slight negative shift of Ba<small><sup>2+</sup></small> peaks at the charging state of NVP at 4.3 V, illustrating that the Ba valence could be affected in accompaniment with Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> intercalation/deintercalation. These results strongly corroborate that the as-expected Ba ion has been doped into the NVP structure, affecting the chemical environment of NVP.</p>
      <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig5"><a href="/image/article/2024/NR/d3nr04300b/d3nr04300b-f5_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d3nr04300b-f5.tif" src="/image/article/2024/NR/d3nr04300b/d3nr04300b-f5.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 5 </b> <span id="fig5"><span class="graphic_title">XPS spectra of (a and b) C 1s, (c and d) V 2p and (e and f) Ba 3d for (a, c and e) NVP and (b, d and f) NVBP-2.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      <p class="otherpara">
        <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig6">Fig. 6(a) and (b)</a> present GCD profiles of NVP and NVBP-2 electrodes at various C rates in a potential range of 2.5–4.3 V <span class="italic">versus</span> Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/Na, respectively. Here, 1C equals the theoretical capacity (117 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>) of NVP. Both electrodes exhibit the typical potential plateau of the NVP cathode at around 3.4 V, corresponding to the redox reaction couple between V<small><sup>4+</sup></small> and V<small><sup>3+</sup></small>.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit40"><sup><span class="sup_ref">40</span></sup></a> The NVBP-2 electrode possesses a relatively superior specific discharge capacity at low C rate (&lt;2C) or high C rate (&gt;2C) when compared with the NVP electrode. The NVBP-2 electrode delivers a specific capacity of 104.5 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> at 0.2C rate and maintains 65.1 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> at 50C. In contrast, the NVP electrode possesses 113.3 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> of specific capacity at 0.2C and there is only 37 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> at 50C. It is noted that the bended profile of the charging/discharge curve at 50C is mainly attributed to the low data collection amount. From potential plateau analysis, it is noted that when the C rate exceeds 2C, there is a huge polarization phenomenon for the NVP electrode but no obvious polarization for the NVBP-2 electrode, indicating the shortened diffusion length of the Na ion and improved redox kinetics after Ba-doping.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit41"><sup><span class="sup_ref">41</span></sup></a> From the rate capability performance (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig6">Fig. 6(c)</a>), the NVBP-2 electrode exhibits superior rate capability compared to NVP and other NVBP electrodes at high current densities (&gt;5C), although the specific capacity of NVP is higher than that of NVBP-2 at 0.2C, 0.5C, 1C and 2C rates. For cycling performance at 0.2C (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig6">Fig. 6(d)</a>), the NVBP-2 electrode also presents a high capacity retention (95.8%) compared to other Ba-doped electrodes after 100 cycles. With further increasing the C rate to 5C and prolonging the cycling span to 500 cycles, as shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig6">Fig. 6(e)</a>, the NVBP-2 electrode presents an excellent capacity retention of 91.5% when compared to 83.2% of NVP, 95.6% of NVBP-1, 93.2% of NVBP-4, and 93.4% of NVBP-8. The high-rate capacity retention has been improved by the suitable Ba-doping content while the low-rate capacity would be damaged. The reasons are unclear and still under investigation.</p>
      <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig6"><a href="/image/article/2024/NR/d3nr04300b/d3nr04300b-f6_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d3nr04300b-f6.tif" src="/image/article/2024/NR/d3nr04300b/d3nr04300b-f6.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 6 </b> <span id="fig6"><span class="graphic_title">GCD profiles of the NVP electrode (a) and NVBP-2 electrode (b) at various rates; cycling capability of NVP and all NVBP electrodes at different rates (c); and cycling stability of NVP and all NVBP electrodes at 0.2C (d) and 5C (e).</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      <p class="otherpara">Since the high rate capability is an important factor in determining the practical value of cathode materials for SIBs, we also detected the high rate capability of the NVBP-2 electrode at 10C and 50C rates. As displayed in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig7">Fig. 7(a)</a>, the electrode possesses 101.5 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> of discharge capacity in the initial charge–discharge test at 10C rate, and the capacity maintains at 97.6 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> at the 500<small><sup>th</sup></small> cycle, while it still maintains at 92.1 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> after 1000 cycles. When tested at 50C rate, the NVBP-2 electrode gives 65.1 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> of specific capacity in the first discharge cycle and maintains 63.2 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> at the 900<small><sup>th</sup></small> discharge cycle, suggesting an excellent cycling stability. The observable voltage steps may be caused by the low data collection rate. Interestingly, the targeted NVBP-2 electrode can still remain with a discharge capacity of 52.8 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> with 81.2% retention after 2000 cycles at 50C in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig7">Fig. 7(b)</a>. However, the NVP electrode only possesses a discharge capacity of 10 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> at 50C rate. This result proves that the structural stability of NVP has been clearly improved by Ba-doping. Simultaneously, after Ba-doping into NVP, the lower migration barrier of Na ions benefits the diffusion of Na ions, especially under high rate conditions. As summarized, this Ba-doping NVP material demonstrates relatively higher rate capability than those of other structural NVP materials (Table S1, ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a>). Two possible reasons are responsible for enhanced electrochemical properties: (1) the inactive Ba-doping in the V-site in NVP stabilizes the NASICON structure during Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> intercalation/de-intercalation due to the stronger Ba–O bond compared to the V–O bond; (2) the appropriate Ba-doping induces the formation of V valence from +3 to +4, which could enable more multi-electron redox reactions. However, excess Ba-doping might lead to lattice distortion and block the diffusion route of Na ions, resulting in poor electrochemical performance.</p>
      <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig7"><a href="/image/article/2024/NR/d3nr04300b/d3nr04300b-f7_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d3nr04300b-f7.tif" src="/image/article/2024/NR/d3nr04300b/d3nr04300b-f7.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 7 </b> <span id="fig7"><span class="graphic_title">Long-term cycling stability of the NVBP-2 electrode at 10C (a) and 50C rates (b).</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      <p class="otherpara">To further deeply understand the electrode reaction kinetic mechanism of the NVP material before and after Ba-doping, CV curves were recorded, and the results are shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig8">Fig. 8(a and b)</a>. The well-defined sharp redox peaks at 3.52/3.49 V should be ascribed to the Na ion intercalation and de-intercalation in the NVP architecture. With the increasing scan rate, NVP and NVBP-2 electrodes present increased the current density and potential polarization. However, the cathodic and anodic peaks of NVP shift significantly towards lower and higher potentials, respectively, due to the enhanced polarization. In contrast, the NVBP-2 electrode still maintains complete redox peaks at a higher scanning rate of 0.5 mV s<small><sup>−1</sup></small>, suggesting faster kinetics and lower polarization. The corresponding linear relationship between peak current and square root of scan rate of the anodic/cathodic process is shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig8">Fig. 8(c)</a>. Calculated on the basis of the Randles–Sevcik equation, the Na ion diffusion coefficient in the solid state NVBP-2 electrode reaches 2.89 × 10<small><sup>−12</sup></small> cm<small><sup>2</sup></small> s<small><sup>−1</sup></small>, which is higher than that of the NVP electrode (1.18 × 10<small><sup>−11</sup></small> cm<small><sup>2</sup></small> s<small><sup>−1</sup></small>). Simultaneously, as shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig8">Fig. 8(d)</a>, in combination with the equation <span class="italic">i</span> = <span class="italic">aυ</span><small><sup><span class="italic">b</span></sup></small> (where <span class="italic">i</span> is the current response, <span class="italic">a</span> is a constant, <span class="italic">υ</span> is the sweep rate and <span class="italic">b</span> is the power-law exponent), the <span class="italic">b</span> value determines the controlled step in the electrochemical process, where <span class="italic">a</span> value close to 0.5 represents a diffusion-controlled step while <span class="italic">a</span> value close to 1 indicates the capacitive behavior of the surface faradaic redox reaction. The calculation results show that NVP has a <span class="italic">b</span> value of 0.39/0.01 for the anodic/cathodic peak current, while NVBP-2 shows a <span class="italic">b</span> value of 0.59/0.32, suggesting that the charge transfer mechanism is limited for NVP, and there is more contribution of the capacitive behavior for NVBP-2. Moreover, according to the calculations based on the equation <span class="italic">i</span>(<span class="italic">V</span>) = <span class="italic">k</span><small><sub>1</sub></small><span class="italic">υ</span> + <span class="italic">k</span><small><sub>2</sub></small><span class="italic">υ</span><small><sup>1/2</sup></small> (where <span class="italic">i</span>(<span class="italic">V</span>) is the response current, <span class="italic">υ</span> is the scan rate, <span class="italic">k</span><small><sub>1</sub></small><span class="italic">υ</span> is the surface capacitive contribution and <span class="italic">k</span><small><sub>2</sub></small><span class="italic">υ</span><small><sup>1/2</sup></small> is a diffusion-controlled process), the proportion of diffusion and surface contribution can be identified. With the increase of the scan rate, the NVBP-2 electrode demonstrates higher capacitive contribution than that of the NVP electrode, which may be attributed to the smaller particle size of NVBP-2 and Ba-doping influence.</p>
      <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig8"><a href="/image/article/2024/NR/d3nr04300b/d3nr04300b-f8_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d3nr04300b-f8.tif" src="/image/article/2024/NR/d3nr04300b/d3nr04300b-f8.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 8 </b> <span id="fig8"><span class="graphic_title">CV curves and corresponding linear relationship of the NVP electrode (a and c) and NVBP-2 electrode (b and d) at various scanning rates.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      <p class="otherpara">EIS tests for NVP and NVBP-2 electrodes were conducted, and the results are shown in Fig. S3.<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a> It is noted that both electrodes show similar semicircles in the high-frequency section and a linear line in the low-frequency section. The semicircle in the high-frequency region represents the charge-transfer resistance (<span class="italic">R</span><small><sub>ct</sub></small>), while the linear line in the low-frequency region indicates the Na ion diffusion procedure during the intercalation/de-intercalation process (<span class="italic">W</span><small><sub>0</sub></small>). In comparison, the NVBP-2 electrode exhibits lower <span class="italic">R</span><small><sub>ct</sub></small> (122.3 Ω) and a relatively more vertical linear line than that of NVP (<span class="italic">R</span><small><sub>ct</sub></small> = 389.2 Ω), which confirms the higher electrochemical reaction activity and faster Na ion diffusion kinetic behavior than that of the NVP electrode.</p>
      <p class="otherpara">To further reveal the regulation of the Na ion diffusion route and electrochemical conversion barrier for the Ba-doped NVP material, the optimized crystal structure of NVP is shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig9">Fig. 9(a)</a>. In the asymmetric unit, there is one V and one P atom, while other atoms (Na and O) are represented in two distinct positions each. The main building block involves two VO<small><sub>6</sub></small> octahedra interlinked by three phosphate groups lying along the <span class="italic">c</span> axis, with sodium atoms located in the voids of the framework with six- (Na1 site) and eight-fold (Na2 site) coordinations. As it is widely known, the Na2 site has higher electrochemical activity than the Na1 site due to the difference in the oxygen environment.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit42"><sup><span class="sup_ref">42,43</span></sup></a> Depending on the projection site of the V atom onto the <span class="italic">a</span>–<span class="italic">b</span> plane, there are three types of V atoms, and <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig9">Fig. 9(b–d)</a> shows Ba-doping into the three different V sites. Additionally, we performed NEB calculations to study the diffusivity of Na ions in Na<small><sub>3</sub></small>V<small><sub>2</sub></small>(PO<small><sub>4</sub></small>)<small><sub>3</sub></small> and NaV<small><sub>2</sub></small>(PO<small><sub>4</sub></small>)<small><sub>3</sub></small>.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit44"><sup><span class="sup_ref">44</span></sup></a><a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig10">Fig. 10(a)</a> illustrates the Na ion migration path from a Na1 site to another Na1 site <span class="italic">via</span> a Na2 site, while <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig10">Fig. 10(b)</a> shows Na ion migration barriers in Na<small><sub>3</sub></small>V<small><sub>2</sub></small>(PO<small><sub>4</sub></small>)<small><sub>3</sub></small> and NaV<small><sub>2</sub></small>(PO<small><sub>4</sub></small>)<small><sub>3</sub></small> of 764 and 311 meV, respectively. This former value is much higher than the latter one, and therefore the Na ion at the Na1 site of NaV<small><sub>2</sub></small>(PO<small><sub>4</sub></small>)<small><sub>3</sub></small> is hard to migrate to another Na1 site.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit45"><sup><span class="sup_ref">45</span></sup></a> In NaV<small><sub>2</sub></small>(PO<small><sub>4</sub></small>)<small><sub>3</sub></small>, the energy at the Na2 site is 665 meV, which is higher than that in Na<small><sub>3</sub></small>V<small><sub>2</sub></small>(PO<small><sub>4</sub></small>)<small><sub>3</sub></small> (33 meV). This energy difference is attributed to destabilization of the Na2 site. One Na ion at the Na1/2 site is repelled by two/four neighboring V ions, and the oxidation states of V in NaV<small><sub>2</sub></small>(PO<small><sub>4</sub></small>)<small><sub>3</sub></small> and Na<small><sub>3</sub></small>V<small><sub>2</sub></small>(PO<small><sub>4</sub></small>)<small><sub>3</sub></small> are +4 and +3, respectively. Therefore, the Na2 site in NaV<small><sub>2</sub></small>(PO<small><sub>4</sub></small>)<small><sub>3</sub></small> is less stable due to the stronger electrostatic repulsion from V ions (+4).<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit46"><sup><span class="sup_ref">46</span></sup></a> With the same calculation strategy for Ba-doping NVP, Na ion migration barriers in Na<small><sub>3</sub></small>V<small><sub>11/12</sub></small>Ba<small><sub>1/12</sub></small>(PO<small><sub>4</sub></small>)<small><sub>3</sub></small> and NaV<small><sub>11/12</sub></small>Ba<small><sub>1/12</sub></small>(PO<small><sub>4</sub></small>)<small><sub>3</sub></small> were found to be 694 and 243 meV, respectively. The lower migration barriers of Na ions after Ba-doping, particularly the one for NaV<small><sub>11/12</sub></small>Ba<small><sub>1/12</sub></small>(PO<small><sub>4</sub></small>)<small><sub>3</sub></small>, shows that the third Na ion along with other two Na ions is more active during the charge/discharge process. To further investigate the origin of lower migration barriers, <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig11">Fig. 11</a> shows the local density distribution near one Ba-doping V site (the charge density distribution is shown in Fig. S4<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1">†</a>). The electronic distribution of V is “[Ar] 3d<small><sup>3</sup></small> 4s<small><sup>2</sup></small>”, while that of Ba is “[Xe] 6s<small><sup>2</sup></small>”. Without the 3d orbital, Ba is less chemically electrophilic, and thus more isosurfaces of electron accumulation appear around the Ba site, as shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig11">Fig. 11</a>. These newly formed electron accumulation surfaces offer more potential paths for Na ion diffusion, which is consistent with previous experimental results. While the accessible Na ion sites of the surface component can be extended to have continuous three-dimensional configurations by allowing the Na ion to diffuse through tetrahedral sites in the framework. This Na ion percolation network with its high connectivity of the diffusion pathway helps to explain the high rate capability of NVBP-2, which is also confirmed by topology analysis. After Ba-doping, the inversion symmetry has been broken due to the non-uniform distribution of V sites along the lattice vector <a href="/image/article/2024/NR/d3nr04300b/d3nr04300b-t1_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d3nr04300b-t1.tif" src="/image/article/2024/NR/d3nr04300b/d3nr04300b-t1.gif"></a> through the disappearance of inversion centers. It is believed that destroying the inversion symmetry can enhance the stability of the crystal lattice without polarization, which benefits Na ion diffusion and improves the electrochemical rate capability of this Ba-doping material.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit47"><sup><span class="sup_ref">47,48</span></sup></a> The lack of such inversion centers leads to the asymmetric polarization of the electric system, where orbitals V<small><sub>3d</sub></small> + V<small><sub>4p</sub></small> and V<small><sub>3d</sub></small> + O<small><sub>2p</sub></small> hybridize, and this leads to a possible 1s-3d transition,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit49"><sup><span class="sup_ref">49</span></sup></a> which exquisitely reveals edge differences between NVP and NVBP phases and explains the improved high-rate performance of the NVBP electrode.</p>
      <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig9"><a href="/image/article/2024/NR/d3nr04300b/d3nr04300b-f9_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d3nr04300b-f9.tif" src="/image/article/2024/NR/d3nr04300b/d3nr04300b-f9.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 9 </b> <span id="fig9"><span class="graphic_title">Optimized crystal structure of the NVP unit cell: (a) and (b) show atomic structures before and after barium replacing the vanadium site, respectively. (c) and (d) show atomic structures after barium replacing Na1 and Na2 sites, respectively.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig10"><a href="/image/article/2024/NR/d3nr04300b/d3nr04300b-f10_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d3nr04300b-f10.tif" src="/image/article/2024/NR/d3nr04300b/d3nr04300b-f10.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 10 </b> <span id="fig10"><span class="graphic_title">Migration path (a) and migration barriers (b) of Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> ions in Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>V<small><sub>2</sub></small>(PO<small><sub>4</sub></small>)<small><sub>3</sub></small> (<span class="italic">x</span> = 1 and 3).</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig11"><a href="/image/article/2024/NR/d3nr04300b/d3nr04300b-f11_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d3nr04300b-f11.tif" src="/image/article/2024/NR/d3nr04300b/d3nr04300b-f11.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 11 </b> <span id="fig11"><span class="graphic_title">Local charge density distribution in (a) NVP and (b) NVBP. The isosurface (yellow and blue) of charge density is plotted at an isovalue of 0.001/<span class="italic">a</span><small><sub>0</sub></small><small><sup>3</sup></small> (<span class="italic">a</span><small><sub>0</sub></small> is the Bohr radius), and the yellow and blue regions represent electron accumulation and depletion, respectively. The site where Ba replaces V is indicated by the black arrow.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
    
    
      
      <h2 id="sect584"><span class="a_heading">3. Conclusion</span></h2>
      <span>In conclusion, a series of Ba-doped Na<small><sub>3</sub></small>V<small><sub>2</sub></small>(PO<small><sub>4</sub></small>)<small><sub>3</sub></small> composites have been synthesized using a sol–gel method following high-temperature sintering treatment. The experimental results demonstrated that Ba-doping into the V-site can significantly improve the electron conductivity, affect the chemical micro-environment of vanadium, and generate an improvement of rate performance and cycling stability. In particular, an insight into the Na ion kinetics was revealed through DFT-assisted topology calculation. It has been found that the enhancement of electrochemical properties is mainly due to the broken inversion symmetry by Ba-doping. The resultant NVBP-2 composite demonstrates a superior electrochemical performance of 104.5 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> at 0.2C, an incredible capacity of 101.5 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> at 10C and 81.6% of capacity retention at 50C over 2000 cycles. This work provides a new viewpoint to screening cathode materials for high sodium storage performance.</span>
